<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Methods</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are optional parameters in methods?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Parameters with default values that callers can omit; the default is substituted at compile time at the call site.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Methods</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">In a hierarchy where <code>Animal a = new Dog();</code>, how can you make <code>a.Category()</code> return the derived value, and what does that imply for API design?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Mark the base member as <code>virtual</code> and the derived member as <code>override</code>. That enables polymorphic dispatch by runtime type. It also means the base API explicitly supports extensibility and behavioral substitution.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Methods</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you prefer <code>new</code> over <code>override</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Prefer <code>new</code> only when polymorphism is not desired and you intentionally want different behavior based on the compile-time reference type (for compatibility or specialized APIs). In most extensible designs, <code>override</code> is the safer default.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Methods</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">A base method is not marked <code>virtual</code>, but you need derived-specific behavior. What are your options?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Option 1: Change the base API to <code>virtual</code> (best if you own the base type and want polymorphism). Option 2: Hide with <code>new</code> (works, but no polymorphism and can confuse callers). Option 3: Redesign with composition/strategy if inheritance is not a good fit.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Methods.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Methods</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a namespace? Why do we need it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A namespace groups related types and prevents naming conflicts by enabling fully qualified names when simple names collide. In daily development, <code>using</code> directives keep call sites readable while preserving unambiguous resolution during compilation. It also improves project structure by mapping code to logical domains.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Namespaces.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Namespaces.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Namespaces</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you prefer file-scoped namespaces over block-scoped namespaces?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Prefer file-scoped namespaces when a file contains one namespace and regular type declarations, because it reduces indentation and visual noise. Use block-scoped form when a file needs multiple namespace blocks or unusual nesting patterns.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Namespaces.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Namespaces.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Namespaces</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is reflection often a bad default in performance-critical code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Reflection shifts work from compile time to runtime: member discovery, argument handling, and dynamic dispatch all add overhead compared to direct calls.<br>
The cost is usually acceptable for startup/configuration paths, but on hot paths it compounds quickly.<br>
Practical rule: cache metadata and use compiled delegates when repeated invocation is required.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Reflection.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Reflection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Reflection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is an attribute and why is reflection central to attribute-driven frameworks?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">An attribute is metadata attached to code elements (types, methods, properties, parameters).<br>
Frameworks inspect these attributes at runtime (or generation time) to apply conventions like routing, validation, serialization, and test discovery.<br>
Without reflection (or generated equivalents), this metadata would remain passive and unused.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Reflection.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Reflection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Reflection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you choose reflection versus alternatives like interfaces, generics, or source generators?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Choose reflection when the shape of types/members is unknown until runtime (plugin ecosystems, late-bound tooling, extensibility points).<br>
Prefer interfaces/generics when contracts are known at compile time because they provide stronger safety and better performance.<br>
Prefer source generators in reflection-heavy infrastructure when you need predictable startup, high throughput, or trim/AOT compatibility.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Reflection.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Reflection.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Reflection</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h5>Types</h5><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between <code>abstract class</code> and <code>interface</code> with default interface methods (C# 8+)? When would you still choose an abstract class?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Both can define contracts with shared implementation. Key differences:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><strong>State</strong>: abstract classes can have instance fields and constructors; interfaces cannot hold instance state (only static fields).</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Inheritance</strong>: a class can implement many interfaces but inherit from only one class.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Access modifiers</strong>: abstract classes support <code>protected</code>/<code>internal</code> members; interface members are implicitly public (C# 8+ allows explicit modifiers but no <code>protected</code> instance state).</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Performance</strong>: virtual dispatch on abstract class methods is a single vtable lookup; default interface methods may involve additional dispatch overhead.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Use Case</strong>: The default implementation for interface methods have different goal compared to the abstract class implemented methods. While in class, semantically methods providing the basic shared functionality for the delivered classes, that don't have to be overriden. While default implementation exist for making extending the interfaces without breaking the inheritors.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can a static class implement an interface? Why or why not?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. A static class compiles to an <code>abstract sealed</code> class at IL level — it cannot be instantiated, so there is no object to dispatch interface calls through. Interfaces require an instance for virtual dispatch. If you need a "static implementation" of a contract, the patterns are:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use static abstract/virtual interface members (C# 11+) with generics: <code>where T : IMyInterface</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use a singleton instance of a regular class that implements the interface.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use delegates/<code>Func&lt;T&gt;</code> instead of an interface.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">A <code>sealed override</code> stops further overriding, but can a derived class use <code>new</code> to hide the sealed method? What happens at runtime?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Yes, <code>new</code> compiles and hides the sealed method. But the behavior depends on the variable's compile-time type:</p>
<pre class="language-csharp"><code class="language-csharp is-loaded"><span class="token keyword">class</span> <span class="token class-name">Base</span> <span class="token punctuation">{</span> <span class="token keyword">public</span> <span class="token keyword">virtual</span> <span class="token return-type class-name"><span class="token keyword">void</span></span> <span class="token function">Do</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span> <span class="token operator">=&gt;</span> Console<span class="token punctuation">.</span><span class="token function">WriteLine</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token string">"Base"</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span> <span class="token punctuation">}</span>
<span class="token keyword">class</span> <span class="token class-name">Middle</span> <span class="token punctuation">:</span> <span class="token type-list"><span class="token class-name">Base</span></span> <span class="token punctuation">{</span> <span class="token keyword">public</span> <span class="token keyword">sealed</span> <span class="token keyword">override</span> <span class="token return-type class-name"><span class="token keyword">void</span></span> <span class="token function">Do</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span> <span class="token operator">=&gt;</span> Console<span class="token punctuation">.</span><span class="token function">WriteLine</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token string">"Middle"</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span> <span class="token punctuation">}</span>
<span class="token keyword">class</span> <span class="token class-name">Bottom</span> <span class="token punctuation">:</span> <span class="token type-list"><span class="token class-name">Middle</span></span> <span class="token punctuation">{</span> <span class="token keyword">public</span> <span class="token keyword">new</span> <span class="token return-type class-name"><span class="token keyword">void</span></span> <span class="token function">Do</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span> <span class="token operator">=&gt;</span> Console<span class="token punctuation">.</span><span class="token function">WriteLine</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token string">"Bottom"</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span> <span class="token punctuation">}</span>

<span class="token class-name">Bottom</span> b <span class="token operator">=</span> <span class="token keyword">new</span> <span class="token constructor-invocation class-name">Bottom</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span>
b<span class="token punctuation">.</span><span class="token function">Do</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span>           <span class="token comment">// "Bottom" — resolved at compile time as Bottom.Do</span>
<span class="token class-name">Middle</span> m <span class="token operator">=</span> b<span class="token punctuation">;</span>
m<span class="token punctuation">.</span><span class="token function">Do</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span>           <span class="token comment">// "Middle" — virtual dispatch resolves to Middle.Do (sealed)</span>
<span class="token class-name">Base</span> x <span class="token operator">=</span> b<span class="token punctuation">;</span>
x<span class="token punctuation">.</span><span class="token function">Do</span><span class="token punctuation">(</span><span class="token punctuation">)</span><span class="token punctuation">;</span>           <span class="token comment">// "Middle" — same virtual dispatch</span>
</code><button class="copy-code-button"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-copy"><rect x="8" y="8" width="14" height="14" rx="2" ry="2"></rect><path d="M4 16c-1.1 0-2-.9-2-2V4c0-1.1.9-2 2-2h10c1.1 0 2 .9 2 2"></path></svg></button></pre>
<p dir="auto">The <code>new</code> method is completely invisible to polymorphic code. This is almost always a design smell — if you need to change behavior, the method should not have been sealed, or you should use composition instead.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can you have an abstract sealed class? What about in IL?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">In C# source code, you cannot write <code>abstract sealed class</code> — the compiler rejects it as contradictory. However, at the IL level, <strong>static classes</strong> compile to exactly <code>abstract sealed</code>. The CLR treats <code>abstract sealed</code> as "cannot be instantiated and cannot be inherited." So every <code>static class</code> in C# is literally an <code>abstract sealed</code> class in metadata. You can verify this with <code>ildasm</code> or reflection: <code>typeof(Math).IsAbstract &amp;&amp; typeof(Math).IsSealed</code> is <code>true</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can <code>partial</code> be dangerous with source generators? Give a concrete scenario.</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Partial classes merge at compile time, and source generators can add members to your type that you do not see in your source file. Dangers:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Name collisions</strong>: a generator adds a method <code>Validate()</code> and you also write <code>Validate()</code> — compile error with a confusing message pointing at generated code.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Implicit behavior changes</strong>: a generator adds <code>INotifyPropertyChanged</code> implementation and overrides <code>Equals</code>. Your tests pass locally but break in CI where a different generator version runs.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Debugging opacity</strong>: stepping through code jumps into generated files that may not be in source control.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Partial method removal</strong>: if a partial method (without accessibility modifier) has no implementing declaration, the compiler silently removes all calls to it. If you forget to generate the body, the call vanishes with no warning.<br>
Best practice: always inspect generated output (visible in IDE under Dependencies and Analyzers), and write tests that verify generator-dependent behavior explicitly.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Two classes have identical fields and values. You compare them with <code>==</code>. Why is it <code>false</code>, and what are the three ways to fix it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>==</code> compares references by default for classes — two separate <code>new</code> calls produce different heap objects with different references.<br>
Fixes:</p>
<ol>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Override <code>Equals</code>/<code>GetHashCode</code> and overload <code>==</code>/<code>!=</code></strong> — full manual control, but tedious and error-prone.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Implement <code>IEquatable&lt;T&gt;</code></strong> — avoids boxing in generic code and gives a clean <code>Equals(T)</code> method. Still need to overload <code>==</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Use <code>record class</code> instead</strong> — the compiler generates value-based <code>Equals</code>, <code>GetHashCode</code>, <code>==</code>, and <code>!=</code> automatically. This is the recommended approach for data-carrier types.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></li>
</ol>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">A static constructor throws an exception. What happens on subsequent accesses to that type?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The runtime marks the type as permanently broken. Every subsequent attempt to access any member of the type throws a <code>TypeInitializationException</code> wrapping the original exception — even if the condition that caused the failure has been resolved. The type cannot be re-initialized for the lifetime of the AppDomain (or AssemblyLoadContext in .NET Core). This is why static constructors should be kept minimal and defensive because failures are unrecoverable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Classes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Classes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does a delegate compile to in IL/runtime terms?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A delegate declaration becomes a sealed type derived from <code>System.MulticastDelegate</code> with <code>Invoke</code>, <code>BeginInvoke</code>, and <code>EndInvoke</code> metadata. Delegate instances carry a target object (or null for static methods), a method pointer, and optionally an invocation list. In modern .NET (6+), calling delegate <code>BeginInvoke</code>/<code>EndInvoke</code> is not supported and throws <code>PlatformNotSupportedException</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Delegates.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Delegates.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Delegates</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you isolate failures in a multicast delegate so one bad subscriber does not break the rest?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Iterate <code>GetInvocationList()</code>, cast each entry to the concrete delegate type, invoke in a per-handler <code>try/catch</code>, and optionally aggregate errors. Direct multicast invocation stops at first exception.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Delegates.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Delegates.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Delegates</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is using <code>Func&lt;Task&gt;</code> in multicast pipelines often wrong for async fan-out?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Direct multicast invocation returns only the last task, so earlier handlers can run unobserved. For async fan-out, iterate <code>GetInvocationList()</code> and await each task explicitly (sequentially or with <code>Task.WhenAll</code>), depending on ordering requirements.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Delegates.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Delegates.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Delegates</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How is an event different from a delegate field in terms of access control?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">An event exposes only <code>add</code>/<code>remove</code> from outside the declaring type. A delegate field can be invoked, replaced, or nulled by external callers. Events preserve publisher ownership of invocation.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Events</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is <code>PriceChanged?.Invoke(this, e)</code> preferred over <code>if (PriceChanged != null) PriceChanged(this, e)</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The null-conditional form avoids the race where another thread unsubscribes between check and call. It uses a copied delegate reference for the invocation expression.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Events</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do event leaks happen, and how do you prevent them?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The publisher keeps strong references to subscriber handlers. If the publisher outlives subscribers, those subscribers cannot be garbage-collected. Prevent with explicit unsubscribe (<code>Dispose</code>), weak-event pattern, or scoped subscription helpers.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Events</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Is <code>EventArgs</code> inheritance mandatory in modern .NET?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. In modern .NET, <code>EventHandler&lt;T&gt;</code> no longer requires <code>T : EventArgs</code>. You can use any payload type, but <code>EventArgs</code>-based design remains the most idiomatic and interoperable style.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Events</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you handle exceptions in event subscribers without losing later handlers?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Copy the invocation list using <code>GetInvocationList()</code> and invoke handlers individually in <code>try/catch</code>. Direct event invocation stops at first exception.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Events.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Events</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">In <code>record Wrapper(List&lt;int&gt; Items)</code>, if <code>var b = a with { };</code> and an item is added to <code>b.Items</code>, does <code>a</code> observe the change, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Yes — <code>a</code> sees the change. <code>with</code> performs a <em>shallow copy</em>: it copies references, not the underlying objects. Both <code>a.Items</code> and <code>b.Items</code> point to the same <code>List&lt;int&gt;</code> instance. Furthermore, <code>a == b</code> was <code>true</code> before the mutation (same reference in both), but the equality check still uses <code>List&lt;T&gt;.Equals</code> which is reference equality — so it remains <code>true</code> even after the content changes. To get proper deep value semantics, use immutable collections (<code>ImmutableList&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>ImmutableArray&lt;T&gt;</code>) or override <code>Equals</code> to compare content.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Records</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When would you choose <code>record class</code> over <code>readonly record struct</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Choose <code>record class</code> when:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The data contains variable-length reference types (strings, collections) — the struct would not avoid heap allocation anyway.</li>
<li dir="auto">The type participates in an inheritance hierarchy (only record classes support inheritance).</li>
<li dir="auto">The data is large (more than ~16 bytes of value-type fields) — copy cost outweighs GC cost.</li>
<li dir="auto">You need <code>null</code> semantics (e.g. optional return values without <code>Nullable&lt;T&gt;</code>).</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Choose <code>readonly record struct</code> when:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The data is small and all fields are value types — avoids heap allocation entirely.</li>
<li dir="auto">You are on a hot path where GC pressure matters (e.g. tight loops, high-throughput pipelines).</li>
<li dir="auto">No inheritance is needed.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Records</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">If <code>Equals</code> on a positional record is overridden to ignore one property, does <code>GetHashCode</code> still include that property, and what breaks?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Yes — the compiler-generated <code>GetHashCode</code> includes all positional properties regardless of your <code>Equals</code> override. This breaks the contract: two objects that are "equal" (by your custom <code>Equals</code>) may have different hash codes, causing them to land in different buckets in <code>Dictionary</code>, <code>HashSet</code>, or any hash-based collection. Lookups silently fail. <strong>Rule</strong>: whenever you override <code>Equals</code>, always override <code>GetHashCode</code> to match. The compiler emits a warning (CS8851) if you override one without the other, but it is only a warning — not an error.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Records</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can a record struct be used as a <code>Dictionary</code> key safely? What do you need to watch out for?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Yes, <code>record struct</code> works well as a dictionary key because the compiler generates value-based <code>Equals</code> and <code>GetHashCode</code> by default. Watch out for:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Mutable record structs</strong> — if a key is mutated after insertion, its hash code changes and it becomes unreachable in the dictionary. Use <code>readonly record struct</code> for keys.</li>
<li dir="auto"><strong>Reference-type properties</strong> — if the record struct contains a reference-type property (e.g. <code>string[]</code>), the generated <code>GetHashCode</code> calls that property's <code>GetHashCode</code>, which for arrays is reference-based (not content-based). Two structurally identical keys with different array instances will hash differently. Override <code>GetHashCode</code> or use immutable value-semantic collections.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Records.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Records</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you choose <code>StringBuilder</code> over <code>string</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>StringBuilder</code> for iterative construction (loops, batched appends, streaming transforms) where many intermediate strings would otherwise be allocated.</li>
<li dir="auto">Prefer interpolation/concatenation for one-off formatting with a small number of values because readability is usually better.</li>
<li dir="auto">In hot paths, benchmark both options and pre-size <code>StringBuilder</code> capacity to reduce buffer growth and copying.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Strings.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Strings.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Strings</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can <code>ReferenceEquals(a, b)</code> be <code>false</code> even when <code>a == b</code> is <code>true</code> for strings?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><code>==</code> for strings compares content, while <code>ReferenceEquals</code> checks object identity.</li>
<li dir="auto">Two strings can contain identical text but be different objects (for example, literal vs runtime-composed value).</li>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>ReferenceEquals</code> only for diagnostics/allocation analysis, not for business equality logic.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Strings.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Strings.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Strings</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How should string comparisons be written in production code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Always call APIs that accept <code>StringComparison</code> (<code>string.Equals</code>, <code>StartsWith</code>, <code>EndsWith</code>, <code>IndexOf</code>) instead of culture-implicit overloads.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>Ordinal</code>/<code>OrdinalIgnoreCase</code> for identifiers, protocol values, keys, and security-sensitive comparisons.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use culture-aware comparison only for user-facing natural-language text where locale behavior is expected.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep the comparison policy explicit and consistent across reads/writes to avoid cross-locale bugs.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Strings.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Strings.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Strings</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">For a high-throughput service processing 100k messages per second (Id, Timestamp, Status), would class or struct be the better model, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A <code>readonly struct</code> (or <code>readonly record struct</code>) is the best fit:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Total payload is roughly 28 bytes before alignment (Guid 16 + DateTime 8 + enum ~4), so it is above the strict 16-byte heuristic and should be benchmarked in your workload.</li>
<li dir="auto">Value semantics avoid a separate object allocation per message when values stay inline and are not boxed/captured, reducing GC pressure at 100k/s.</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>readonly</code> prevents accidental mutation.</li>
<li dir="auto">If the message grows or needs richer behavior/reference sharing, a class can become the better tradeoff.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Structs.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Structs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Structs</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does mutating a struct returned from a property or indexer not compile (or silently do nothing)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Property/indexer access usually returns a value copy, not a reference to the original storage.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mutating that copy would be discarded immediately, so the compiler blocks common forms (for example CS1612 scenarios).</li>
<li dir="auto">Even when a pattern compiles, mutations can affect only the temporary copy, not the original struct instance.</li>
<li dir="auto">Mitigate with <code>readonly struct</code> for safer value semantics, or expose <code>ref</code>/<code>ref readonly</code> returns when true by-reference behavior is required.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Structs.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Structs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Structs</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does <code>ValueType.Equals</code> perform so poorly on structs with reference-type fields? What should you do about it?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Default value-type equality is field-by-field and can be expensive in hot paths.</li>
<li dir="auto">Depending on type shape/runtime path, equality can include reflection-like overhead and extra indirection.</li>
<li dir="auto">Using such structs as keys in dictionaries/sets amplifies the cost because equality and hashing are called frequently.</li>
<li dir="auto">Implement <code>IEquatable&lt;T&gt;</code> and override <code>Equals</code>/<code>GetHashCode</code> to define stable semantics and avoid hidden performance costs.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Structs.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Structs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Structs</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can updating a value-type item inside <code>foreach</code> fail to persist, and what are safe fixes?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">The loop variable was a copy of a value type, so mutations were applied to the copy.</li>
<li dir="auto">The same issue appears when mutating structs returned by properties, because property access often returns a copy.</li>
<li dir="auto">Fix by making the struct immutable and replacing whole values, or by redesigning to avoid mutable structs in those paths.</li>
<li dir="auto">If mutation is required, use APIs with explicit <code>ref</code> semantics very carefully.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Types.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Types.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Types</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Where does boxing usually sneak in, and what is the practical mitigation in production code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Boxing happens when a value type is converted to <code>object</code> or interface-typed APIs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Each boxing operation allocates and can increase GC pressure in hot paths.</li>
<li dir="auto">Prefer generic APIs (<code>List&lt;T&gt;</code>, <code>EqualityComparer&lt;T&gt;</code>, generic interfaces) so calls stay strongly typed.</li>
<li dir="auto">Verify with profiling before optimizing, then remove high-frequency boxing boundaries.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Types.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Types.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Types</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What criteria should drive choosing between <code>struct</code>, <code>class</code>, and <code>record class</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>struct</code> for tiny immutable value objects when copy semantics are desired and boxing is controlled.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>class</code> for identity-rich entities where reference identity and lifecycle matter.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use <code>record class</code> for data-centric models where value-based equality improves correctness.</li>
<li dir="auto">Validate the choice against mutation rules, size/copy costs, and equality requirements.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Types.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/CSharp/Fundamentals/Types/Types.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Types</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>Other</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What problem did OWIN solve?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">It standardized the server-application boundary so middleware/app components were portable across hosts.</li>
<li dir="auto">It enabled a composable pipeline model independent of <code>System.Web</code> internals.</li>
<li dir="auto">It reduced host lock-in for teams maintaining ASP.NET-era applications.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/OWIN.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/OWIN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OWIN</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Is ASP.NET Core OWIN?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">No. ASP.NET Core is not OWIN, even though both use middleware pipelines.</li>
<li dir="auto">ASP.NET Core uses different abstractions (<code>HttpContext</code>, endpoint routing, hosting model) and has first-class integration with modern .NET runtime/tooling.</li>
<li dir="auto">Migration should be treated as conceptual reuse plus API rewrite, not a drop-in protocol swap.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/OWIN.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/OWIN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OWIN</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How should incremental migration vs full rewrite be chosen when modernizing an OWIN app under strict uptime requirements?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Prefer incremental migration when downtime risk and release pressure are high; isolate seams (auth, API endpoints, cross-cutting middleware) and move components in slices.</li>
<li dir="auto">Prefer rewrite when current architecture blocks critical goals (performance, security posture, operability) and business can fund a transition window.</li>
<li dir="auto">Decide with hard constraints: SLA tolerance, test coverage quality, team expertise, and ability to run parallel environments safely.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/OWIN.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/OWIN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">OWIN</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is SignalR a good fit?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Use SignalR when clients need server-pushed updates with low latency (chat, collaboration, live telemetry).</li>
<li dir="auto">It is most valuable when update frequency is high enough that polling wastes bandwidth or increases staleness.</li>
<li dir="auto">If updates are rare and latency tolerance is high, simpler HTTP polling can be cheaper to operate.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/SignalR.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/SignalR.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SignalR</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the first scaling problem you will hit?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Cross-node message fan-out: messages sent on one server do not automatically reach clients connected to another node.</li>
<li dir="auto">Plan scale-out early with Azure SignalR Service or a supported backplane, then validate routing under load tests.</li>
<li dir="auto">Also validate sticky-session requirements for your chosen topology and transport strategy.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/SignalR.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/SignalR.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SignalR</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why are SignalR groups not enough for authorization?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Groups control message routing, not permission checks.</li>
<li dir="auto">Membership can change/rejoin over reconnect paths, so relying on groups alone risks privilege drift.</li>
<li dir="auto">Enforce security with authentication and policy/role-based authorization on hub methods.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/SignalR.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Other/SignalR.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SignalR</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h3>Runtime</h3><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is managed vs unmanaged code?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Managed code runs under the .NET runtime (CLR) and benefits from runtime services like type safety checks, exception handling, garbage collection, and JIT/AOT compilation.<br>
Unmanaged code runs directly as native machine code under the OS (for example, C/C++ binaries). It does not run under the CLR and typically requires explicit resource and lifetime management.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Common Language Runtime.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Common Language Runtime.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Common Language Runtime</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the CLR? What does it do? What is IL (CIL/MSIL)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The CLR (Common Language Runtime) is the execution engine of .NET. It loads assemblies, verifies and executes IL, compiles IL to native code (JIT or AOT), manages memory (GC), handles exceptions, supports threading and interop, and provides other runtime services.<br>
IL (also called CIL or MSIL) is the CPU-independent intermediate instruction set produced by .NET language compilers and stored in assemblies together with metadata. The CLR turns IL into native code for the current platform.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Common Language Runtime.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Common Language Runtime.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Common Language Runtime</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the Garbage Collector? Why do we need it? How does it work (high level)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The GC is the .NET runtime's automatic memory manager for managed objects. It periodically finds objects that are no longer reachable from GC roots, reclaims their memory, and (on the SOH) typically compacts surviving objects to reduce fragmentation.<br>
The GC is generational (Gen 0/1/2): most collections are small and fast, while full collections are less frequent.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Garbage Collector.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Garbage Collector.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Garbage Collector</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the Small Object Heap (SOH) and the Large Object Heap (LOH)?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The SOH stores most objects (typically smaller than ~85,000 bytes) and is compacted regularly.<br>
The LOH stores large allocations (typically 85,000 bytes and above, often large arrays). It is collected with Gen 2 and can become fragmented; compaction behavior differs from the SOH and is more expensive.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Garbage Collector.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Garbage Collector.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Garbage Collector</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a memory leak?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Memory that is no longer needed but cannot be reclaimed. In .NET this can be caused by keeping objects reachable (managed leaks) or by not releasing unmanaged resources.<br>
See also the Memory Leaks note in this runtime section.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Garbage Collector.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Garbage Collector.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Garbage Collector</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a memory leak? Is it possible in .NET? How?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A memory leak is memory that is no longer needed but cannot be reclaimed, so the process keeps growing over time.<br>
Yes, it is possible in .NET:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Managed leaks: objects stay reachable (for example via static caches, event subscriptions, long-lived collections), so GC cannot collect them.</li>
<li dir="auto">Unmanaged leaks: native memory/handles are allocated (directly or indirectly) and not released (for example, missing <code>Dispose()</code> / <code>using</code>).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Memory Leaks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Memory Leaks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Memory Leaks</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the call stack? Can it overflow? What happens then?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The call stack is a per-thread LIFO memory region that stores stack frames for active method calls (return address, parameters, locals, etc.).<br>
It can overflow (for example, due to deep or infinite recursion or very large stack allocations). In .NET this typically results in <code>StackOverflowException</code>, and the process is terminated (it cannot be reliably handled).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Memory Leaks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Memory Leaks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Memory Leaks</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do we need <code>using {}</code> if there is a GC?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>using</code> provides deterministic cleanup for resources that are not just managed memory (file handles, sockets, OS handles, unmanaged buffers). GC runs non-deterministically and does not guarantee timely release of such resources.<br>
The <code>using</code> statement compiles to <code>try/finally</code> so <code>Dispose()</code> is called even when exceptions occur.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Memory Leaks.md" href="Software Engineering/01 Programming/NET/Runtime/Memory Leaks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Memory Leaks</a></em></p>
