</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you reduce cache stampede?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Add jitter to expirations so hot keys do not all expire simultaneously. Use request coalescing (singleflight pattern) so only one caller recomputes while others await the result. Consider stale-while-revalidate or background refresh to avoid blocking on recomputation entirely. <code>HybridCache</code> in .NET 9+ handles coalescing automatically.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you fix stale read-after-write behavior in Redis user-profile caching without turning off caching?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Define the correctness target first: read-your-writes for the updating user, plus a staleness budget for everyone else. On the write path, either update the cached value or delete it after the database commit succeeds. If there are multiple writers or async pipelines, publish an invalidation event and consume it in all app instances. For per-user read-your-writes, use a version token in the cache key (row version or updated_at) so the writer reads the new version immediately. Keep a TTL as a safety net for missed invalidations. Add monitoring: rate of stale reads, invalidation lag, and cache hit ratio per key type.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What changes reduce p99 spikes and database CPU saturation in TTL-only caching with synchronized five-minute expirations?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Add jitter to TTL so expirations spread over time. Add request coalescing so only one request recomputes a key and others await the same result. Consider stale-while-revalidate so you never block on recomputation for hot keys. Add a short circuit to protect the database: rate-limit recomputes, timeouts, and fallback behavior. Identify and treat hot keys separately: shorter payloads, proactive refresh, dedicated cache region.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What should be checked first when a multi-tenant cache leaks one tenant's data to another, and how is it prevented?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Assume a cache key correctness bug until proven otherwise: ensure tenant ID and auth scope are part of every key. Verify there is no shared key for different query parameters, locale, feature flags, or permissions. Ensure you do not cache error pages or partial failures that can later be served as valid results. Use strongly typed key builders and centralized key composition to prevent ad-hoc string keys. Add defense in depth: validate tenant on deserialized payload before returning and log violations.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/Caching.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Caching</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Explain ACID</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">ACID describes key transaction guarantees:</p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Atomicity: all operations in a transaction succeed or none do.</li>
<li dir="auto">Consistency: committed transactions move the database between valid states (respecting constraints).</li>
<li dir="auto">Isolation: concurrent transactions do not observe each other's intermediate states (controlled by isolation levels).</li>
<li dir="auto">Durability: once committed, data survives crashes (via logging, checkpoints, replication, etc.).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ACID.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ACID.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ACID</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="03-data-persistence-nosql">NoSQL</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are non-relational databases?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Non-relational databases (often grouped under the term NoSQL) store and query data without the classic relational model of normalized tables and joins. They typically favor flexible schemas and horizontal scaling, and come in several major families: key-value, document, wide-column, and graph databases.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NoSQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Which NoSQL family fits a user profile API with very frequent reads by user id, and why?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Key value or document store.<br>
Use key value if the access pattern is almost entirely by id and you do not need rich querying.<br>
Use document store if you need to read and update an aggregate document (profile + preferences) and occasionally query by a few indexed fields.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NoSQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is NoSQL a bad idea?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When your core use case needs relational constraints and multi-entity transactions, or your queries are fundamentally join heavy.<br>
In that case, add caching, read replicas, or a denormalized read model before switching storage models.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/NoSQL/NoSQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">NoSQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="03-data-persistence-orms">ORMs</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How to choose Code First vs Database First?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use Code First when you want the code model to be the source of truth (new projects, strong domain model, migrations as part of delivery). Use Database First when you already have a database schema you must integrate with (legacy DB, shared DB, strict DBA-controlled schema) and want to generate the model from it.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Entity Framework</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What inheritance mapping strategies exist in Entity Framework?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The common strategies are: TPH (Table Per Hierarchy, single table with a discriminator), TPT (Table Per Type, separate table per type), and TPC (Table Per Concrete type, separate table per concrete type). They trade off query simplicity, normalization, and performance.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Entity Framework</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you do a backward-compatible schema migration with zero downtime (expand-contract, backfill, dual reads/writes), and what can go wrong?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use an expand-contract migration:<br>
add new schema elements in a backward-compatible way (nullable columns/tables/indexes), deploy code that writes to both old and new (or writes new while keeping old readable), then backfill in batches.<br>
Switch reads behind a feature flag when data is consistent, keep a fallback path temporarily, and only then contract (remove old columns) once all writers/readers are updated.<br>
Failure modes: wrong deploy order, backfill racing with live writes, long-running migrations/locks, missing defaults/null handling, read-model inconsistency, and hard rollbacks after data has diverged.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/Entity Framework.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Entity Framework</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is an ORM?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">An ORM (Object-Relational Mapper) maps relational database tables/rows to objects and translates queries/changes in code into SQL. It helps with CRUD, tracking changes, relationships, and migrations, at the cost of an abstraction layer you must understand to avoid performance issues.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/ORMs.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/ORMs/ORMs.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">ORMs</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="03-data-persistence-sql">SQL</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is normalization?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Normalization is organizing relational data to reduce redundancy and avoid update anomalies by decomposing tables and defining keys/relationships (e.g., 1NF, 2NF, 3NF). The goal is consistency and maintainability, not always maximum read performance.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Normalization Denormalization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is denormalization?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Denormalization is intentionally introducing redundancy (e.g., duplicating fields, adding aggregates/materialized views) to speed up reads and simplify queries. It shifts complexity to writes and requires extra care to keep data consistent.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Normalization Denormalization.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Normalization Denormalization</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is an index and what types exist?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">An index is an additional on-disk/in-memory structure (often a B-tree/B+ tree) that speeds up access via seeks and ordered scans. Common types are clustered and nonclustered indexes, plus unique, composite, filtered/partial, and full-text indexes depending on DB engine.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How does ordering work for clustered vs nonclustered indexes?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">In a clustered index, the leaf level is the table data itself, physically ordered by the clustered key. In a nonclustered index, the leaf level is ordered by nonclustered key and stores locators to data rows.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why cannot a table have two clustered indexes?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A clustered index defines physical row order. One table can be physically ordered only one way at a time.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">If clustered tables are often faster for reads, why would you still use a heap?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Use this for bulk-load/staging scenarios and append-heavy transient data where ordered access is not required.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do fill factor and page splits affect write performance?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Lower fill factor leaves room on pages and can reduce splits, but increases storage and read IO.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can a clustered index key contain duplicates?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Yes. SQL Server makes keys unique internally by adding a hidden uniquifier when needed.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How are rows located from a nonclustered index when the base table is a heap?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">The nonclustered leaf stores a RID pointing to file/page/slot.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why not index every column?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Extra indexes increase write cost, memory pressure, and maintenance; some low-selectivity indexes are not useful.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Do you always make the primary key clustered?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">No. It is common, but choose clustered key by access patterns, size, and update behavior.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between composite and covering indexes?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Composite defines key columns used for seek/order. Covering also includes non-key columns to avoid lookups.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Can you index only a subset of rows?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Yes, with filtered indexes (where supported by engine).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/Indexes.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Indexes</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is normalization? What are its first three normal forms?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Answer is not provided in the source interview list; see Links.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is denormalization? When is it useful?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Answer is not provided in the source interview list; see Links.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the logical SQL query execution order?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ol>
<li dir="auto">FROM</li>
<li dir="auto">ON</li>
<li dir="auto">JOIN</li>
<li dir="auto">WHERE</li>
<li dir="auto">GROUP BY</li>
<li dir="auto">WITH CUBE or WITH ROLLUP</li>
<li dir="auto">HAVING</li>
<li dir="auto">SELECT</li>
<li dir="auto">DISTINCT</li>
<li dir="auto">ORDER BY</li>
<li dir="auto">TOP<br>
A common logical order is: <code>FROM</code> (incl. <code>JOIN</code>/<code>ON</code>) -&gt; <code>WHERE</code> -&gt; <code>GROUP BY</code> -&gt; <code>HAVING</code> -&gt; <code>SELECT</code> -&gt; <code>DISTINCT</code> -&gt; <code>ORDER BY</code> -&gt; <code>TOP</code>/<code>LIMIT</code>/<code>OFFSET</code> (exact details vary by engine).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></li>
</ol>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">In what structure is data stored in SQL?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">In the relational model, data is stored as relations: tables with columns (attributes) and rows (tuples). Tables are grouped into schemas/databases, and relationships are represented via keys (primary/foreign) rather than by nesting objects.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a primary key?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A primary key is a constraint that uniquely identifies each row in a table. It must be unique and not null, can be single-column or composite, and is typically backed by an index (implementation depends on the DB engine).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a foreign key?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A foreign key is a constraint that enforces a relationship between tables by requiring values in one table to reference existing values in a primary/unique key of another table. It protects referential integrity and can define cascading actions on update/delete.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a unique key?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A unique key (UNIQUE constraint) enforces that values in a column (or set of columns) are unique across the table. Unlike a primary key, a table can have multiple unique constraints; null handling varies by DB engine.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What table constraints exist?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Typical constraints include: <code>NOT NULL</code>, <code>CHECK</code>, <code>DEFAULT</code>, <code>UNIQUE</code>, <code>PRIMARY KEY</code>, and <code>FOREIGN KEY</code>. They are used to enforce domain rules, uniqueness, and referential integrity at the database level.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What JOIN types exist?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Common JOINs are: <code>INNER JOIN</code>, <code>LEFT OUTER JOIN</code>, <code>RIGHT OUTER JOIN</code>, <code>FULL OUTER JOIN</code>, and <code>CROSS JOIN</code>. A self-join is when you join a table to itself; semi/anti joins are often expressed via <code>EXISTS</code>/<code>NOT EXISTS</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What does DISTINCT do?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>DISTINCT</code> removes duplicate rows from the result set (uniqueness is evaluated across the selected columns). It is applied after <code>SELECT</code> projection in the logical processing order.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between WHERE and HAVING?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>WHERE</code> filters rows before grouping/aggregation. <code>HAVING</code> filters groups after <code>GROUP BY</code> and can use aggregate expressions like <code>COUNT(*)</code> or <code>SUM(amount)</code>.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between CHAR and VARCHAR?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><code>CHAR(n)</code> is fixed-length: values are padded to length <code>n</code>. <code>VARCHAR(n)</code> is variable-length: it stores only the actual length (plus small overhead). Use <code>NCHAR</code>/<code>NVARCHAR</code> when you need Unicode (engine-specific).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a stored procedure and how is it different from a function?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A stored procedure is server-side code stored in the database and executed by name; it can encapsulate multi-step logic, perform data modifications, and return result sets. Functions usually return a value (scalar/table) and are often more restricted in side effects (varies by DB engine).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a trigger?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A trigger is database code that automatically runs in response to events (e.g., <code>INSERT</code>, <code>UPDATE</code>, <code>DELETE</code>, sometimes DDL events). It is commonly used for enforcing rules, auditing, and maintaining derived data.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a subquery?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A subquery is a query nested inside another query (in <code>SELECT</code>, <code>FROM</code>, or <code>WHERE</code>/<code>HAVING</code>). It can be correlated (depends on outer row) or non-correlated, and can be used for filtering, projection, or building derived tables.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a transaction?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A transaction groups multiple operations into a single unit of work that either fully commits or rolls back. Transactions provide guarantees like atomicity and isolation, controlled by the database's transaction and isolation model.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a Common Table Expression?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A CTE (Common Table Expression) is a named, temporary result set defined with <code>WITH</code> and used within a single statement. It improves readability, enables query composition, and supports recursion for hierarchical data.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" href="Software Engineering/03 Data Persistence/SQL/SQL.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">SQL</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="04-networks">04 Networks</h1><h2 id="04-networks-architecture-ops">Architecture &amp; Ops</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between client VPN and site to site VPN?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Client VPN connects a single device.<br>
Site to site connects two networks.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Architecture &amp; Ops/VPN.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">VPN</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="04-networks-protocols">Protocols</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does DNS "take time" to update?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Caching.<br>
Resolvers and clients respect TTL and keep old answers until they expire.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/DNS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">DNS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between HTTP and HTTPS?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">HTTPS is HTTP over TLS.<br>
It provides confidentiality (encryption), integrity (tamper detection), and server authentication via certificates.<br>
Plain HTTP has no built-in protection against eavesdropping or manipulation.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP &amp; HTTPS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What HTTP methods exist?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Common methods: GET, POST, PUT, PATCH, DELETE, HEAD, OPTIONS.<br>
Less common: TRACE, CONNECT.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP &amp; HTTPS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the difference between GET and POST?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">GET is intended for retrieving a resource representation; it is safe and can be cached.<br>
POST is intended for submitting data to be processed (often creating a new resource or triggering an action); it is not safe and is generally not idempotent.<br>
GET parameters are typically in the URL query; POST usually sends a request body.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP &amp; HTTPS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What do idempotency and safety mean for HTTP methods?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Safe means the request does not change server state (for example, GET, HEAD, OPTIONS).<br>
Idempotent means repeating the same request has the same effect as doing it once (for example, GET, PUT, DELETE).<br>
A method can be idempotent but not safe (for example, DELETE).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP &amp; HTTPS.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP &amp; HTTPS</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What problem does HTTP 2 solve compared to HTTP 1.1?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Multiplexing and better use of one connection.<br>
It reduces the need for many parallel TCP connections.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP 2</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can HTTP 2 still stall under packet loss?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because it still uses TCP.<br>
A lost packet blocks delivery of later packets for all streams in that connection.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/HTTP 2.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">HTTP 2</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">URI vs URL: what is the difference?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">URI (Uniform Resource Identifier) identifies a resource.<br>
URL (Uniform Resource Locator) is a type of URI that also tells you how and where to access the resource (scheme plus location).<br>
Example: <code>https://example.com/a/b?x=1#top</code> is a URL (and therefore a URI).<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/Protocols.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/Protocols.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Protocols</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When is gRPC a better choice than REST?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When you own both ends, need strong contracts, and benefit from streaming or lower overhead.<br>
It is common for internal service to service communication.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">gRPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What are the first two things you set for production gRPC clients?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Deadlines and retry policy.<br>
Without deadlines, resource usage is unbounded.<br>
Without a retry strategy, transient failures turn into user-visible failures.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Protocols/gRPC.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">gRPC</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="04-networks-transport-sockets">Transport &amp; Sockets</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do partial reads happen?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Because TCP is a byte stream.<br>
The network may deliver data in smaller chunks.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/Sockets.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Sockets</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">UDP vs TCP: what is the difference?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">TCP is connection-oriented and provides reliable, ordered delivery with retransmissions, flow control, and congestion control.<br>
UDP is connectionless and sends independent datagrams with no built-in reliability or ordering.<br>
Typical uses: TCP for web traffic and most application protocols; UDP for real-time media, DNS, and protocols that implement reliability themselves.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/TCP IP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/TCP IP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">TCP IP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do some systems prefer UDP?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Lower latency and no connection setup.<br>
The application can decide what to do on loss.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" href="Software Engineering/04 Networks/Transport &amp; Sockets/UDP.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">UDP</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h1 id="05-architecture">05 Architecture</h1><h2 id="05-architecture-application-architecture">Application Architecture</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is multi-layered architecture?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Multi-layered architecture splits the system into layers such as Presentation, Application (use cases), Domain (business rules), and Infrastructure/Data access. Each layer has a focused responsibility and communicates through well-defined interfaces, which improves maintainability and testability.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Layered Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is Onion Architecture?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">Onion Architecture is a layered style where the Domain is at the center and dependencies point inward. Outer layers (infrastructure, UI, frameworks) depend on inner layers; inner layers do not depend on details. This is usually enforced by defining abstractions (interfaces) in the inner layers and implementing them in the outer layers.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/Layered Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Layered Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is MVC and why is it used?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">MVC stands for Model-View-Controller. The Model represents the domain/data and business rules, the View is the UI (rendering), and the Controller handles incoming input/requests, coordinates work, and selects the response/view. The separation helps keep UI concerns out of business logic and makes the system easier to test and evolve.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/MVC MVVM.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Application Architecture/MVC MVVM.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">MVC MVVM</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="05-architecture-architecture-styles">Architecture Styles</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why can microservices lead to distributed data consistency problems, and how do you address them?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Each service owns its data, so cross-service business actions cannot rely on one ACID transaction.</li>
<li dir="auto">A later step can fail after an earlier local commit, producing partial state.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use sagas with compensating actions across local transactions.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use outbox/inbox and idempotent handlers to survive retries and duplicates.</li>
<li dir="auto">Accept eventual consistency and make process state observable.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Microservices.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Microservices.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Microservices</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Order and Inventory are separate microservices. A customer places an order. How do you ensure inventory is reserved without a distributed transaction?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Persist order as <code>Pending</code> and publish <code>OrderPlaced</code> via outbox in the same local transaction.</li>
<li dir="auto">Inventory consumes <code>OrderPlaced</code> and emits <code>InventoryReserved</code> or <code>InventoryRejected</code>.</li>
<li dir="auto">Order transitions to <code>Confirmed</code> or <code>Rejected</code> based on the inventory event.</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep consumers idempotent by tracking processed message IDs.</li>
<li dir="auto">Add timeout compensation if inventory does not respond within SLA.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Microservices.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Microservices.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Microservices</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you decide between monolith, modular monolith, and microservices for a new product?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Decide from team size, release pressure, domain volatility, and ops maturity.</li>
<li dir="auto">One team in discovery phase usually benefits most from monolith speed.</li>
<li dir="auto">Clear domains with limited platform capacity often fit modular monolith.</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose microservices when independent deploy/scale constraints are proven.</li>
<li dir="auto">Re-evaluate architecture periodically as constraints change.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Microservices.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Microservices.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Microservices</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is a modular monolith?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">A monolith with explicit module boundaries, clear dependencies, and internal APIs.<br>
It preserves operational simplicity while controlling coupling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Monolith Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Monolith Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monolith Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When do microservices become justified?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto">When independent deployment is a hard requirement and the team can support the operational complexity.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Monolith Architecture.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Architecture Styles/Monolith Architecture.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Monolith Architecture</a></em></p>
</div></div></div><h2 id="05-architecture-distributed-systems">Distributed Systems</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">A client sends <code>POST /api/orders</code>, times out, and retries. How do you prevent duplicate orders?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<p dir="auto"><strong>Expected answer:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Require an <code>Idempotency-Key</code> header for create operations.</li>
<li dir="auto">Persist key + request hash + resulting order ID with TTL.</li>
<li dir="auto">On retry with same key and same payload, return the original result (<code>201</code> or <code>200</code>) instead of creating a new order.</li>
<li dir="auto">On same key with different payload, reject with <code>409 Conflict</code>.<br>
<strong>Why this matters:</strong> demonstrates reliability under partial failure and correct non-idempotent write handling.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/REST.md" href="Software Engineering/05 Architecture/Distributed Systems/REST.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">REST</a></em></li>
</ul>
