</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Should unsupervised learning be the starting point for customer segmentation when churn labels already exist?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start with a supervised churn baseline to quantify immediate business impact.</li>
<li dir="auto">Use clustering as a complementary lens for exploration and cohort discovery.</li>
<li dir="auto">Test whether cluster features improve supervised metrics or intervention design.</li>
<li dir="auto">Avoid replacing a known target task with unsupervised objectives by default.</li>
<li dir="auto">Why: when labels exist, supervised learning usually maps more directly to outcomes.<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Types/Unsupervised Learning.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Machine Learning/Types/Unsupervised Learning.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Unsupervised Learning</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><h2 id="11-ai-ml-tooling">Tooling</h2><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you choose between a terminal-first coding agent and an IDE-first coding agent?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Start with team workflow: terminal-first fits script-heavy and CI-centric teams, IDE-first fits interactive editing-heavy teams</li>
<li dir="auto">Compare observability: terminal-first often exposes every command clearly, while IDE-first may optimize UX but hide low-level steps</li>
<li dir="auto">Validate integration needs: if your process depends on PR workflows, issue trackers, and editor navigation, IDE integration can reduce friction</li>
<li dir="auto">Check policy controls: hooks, rules files, and approval gates matter more than UI style in regulated or production-sensitive environments<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Tooling.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Tooling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Tooling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why are agent instructions and hooks more important than model quality in long-running repos?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Instructions encode project constraints (architecture, naming, testing expectations) that the base model does not know</li>
<li dir="auto">Hooks create automatic guardrails, catching policy violations before merge</li>
<li dir="auto">Better models can still make wrong local decisions without repo-specific constraints</li>
<li dir="auto">Reliable behavior over months depends on repeatable controls, not one-off prompt quality<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Tooling.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Tooling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Tooling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the practical difference between a coding agent and a code review agent?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Coding agents optimize change creation: plan, edit, run checks, iterate</li>
<li dir="auto">Review agents optimize change evaluation: identify risk, gaps, regressions, and missing tests</li>
<li dir="auto">They complement each other: one increases delivery speed, the other protects quality and maintainability</li>
<li dir="auto">Mature teams use both to keep throughput high without lowering merge standards<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Tooling.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Tooling.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Tooling</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why is an agentic coding tool not just "better autocomplete"?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Autocomplete predicts local tokens, while coding agents execute multi-step plans across files and tools</li>
<li dir="auto">Agents can run verification commands and revise output based on failures, which autocomplete does not do autonomously</li>
<li dir="auto">The mechanism changes risk: agent output must be governed with approvals, tests, and repository rules</li>
<li dir="auto">Net benefit comes from closed-loop execution, not from raw text quality alone<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Coding Agents.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Coding Agents.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Coding Agents</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should a team prefer terminal agents over IDE agents?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Prefer terminal agents when workflows are command-heavy, scriptable, and CI-first</li>
<li dir="auto">Prefer IDE agents when developers rely on interactive navigation, visual diffing, and editor-native refactors</li>
<li dir="auto">The deciding factor is operational fit with current engineering process, not vendor marketing claims</li>
<li dir="auto">Teams can mix both if shared instruction files and validation gates keep behavior consistent<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Coding Agents.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Coding Agents.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Coding Agents</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What controls reduce production risk when adopting coding agents?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Repository instruction files to encode architecture and testing constraints</li>
<li dir="auto">Hooks and policy checks to block unsafe operations automatically</li>
<li dir="auto">Bounded execution: cost limits, step limits, and explicit approval points</li>
<li dir="auto">Mandatory verification (build, tests, lint) before merge, regardless of which model produced the patch<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Coding Agents.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Coding Agents.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Coding Agents</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do skills usually improve agent reliability more than repeating instructions in every prompt?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Skills are persistent and reusable, so important constraints are present every time instead of being forgotten in ad-hoc prompts</li>
<li dir="auto">They standardize behavior across contributors and sessions, reducing output variance</li>
<li dir="auto">Structured frontmatter plus explicit usage guidance improves skill selection and timing</li>
<li dir="auto">Skills are version-controlled artifacts, so teams can review and evolve them like code<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Skills.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Skills.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Skills</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should you choose project-scoped skills over user-global skills?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Choose project scope when conventions are repository-specific and must be shared by all contributors</li>
<li dir="auto">Choose project scope when CI, linters, or architecture rules are tightly coupled to that codebase</li>
<li dir="auto">Use user-global skills for personal productivity preferences that should not override team policy</li>
<li dir="auto">If a rule would surprise teammates in a PR review, it should be project-scoped, not global<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Skills.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Skills.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Skills</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why should destructive-operation controls live in <code>PreToolUse</code> instead of <code>PostToolUse</code>?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto"><code>PreToolUse</code> is the last decision point before side effects, so it can prevent damage</li>
<li dir="auto"><code>PostToolUse</code> runs after execution and cannot reliably undo external side effects</li>
<li dir="auto">Preventive controls produce cleaner failure modes than remediation after the fact</li>
<li dir="auto">A good pattern is deny in pre-hooks, then lint/format/log in post-hooks<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Hooks.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Hooks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Hooks</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">How do you design hook pipelines that improve quality without making the agent unusably slow?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Gate only high-risk actions synchronously (writes, destructive commands, protected paths)</li>
<li dir="auto">Keep post hooks deterministic and incremental (format changed files, not the whole repo)</li>
<li dir="auto">Move expensive checks to commit/CI boundaries or async hook paths</li>
<li dir="auto">Track hook duration and failure rate; prune or split hooks that dominate loop time<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Hooks.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Hooks.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Hooks</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why does MCP reduce integration complexity for agent tooling ecosystems?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Without a shared protocol, each client-service pair needs custom integration code, creating N x M scaling</li>
<li dir="auto">MCP changes this to N + M: each client implements MCP once, each service exposes one MCP server</li>
<li dir="auto">The shared primitives (tools, resources, prompts) standardize capability discovery and invocation</li>
<li dir="auto">Tradeoff: protocol standardization simplifies interoperability but adds runtime/server management overhead<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Plugins.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Plugins.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Plugins</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should a team intentionally limit plugin count even if more integrations are available?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">When accuracy and latency degrade from too many tool schemas in context</li>
<li dir="auto">When the security team cannot properly review or monitor additional server dependencies</li>
<li dir="auto">When task scope is narrow and extra plugins create more selection noise than value</li>
<li dir="auto">Good practice is progressive enablement: start minimal, add only tools that measurably improve outcomes<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Plugins.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Plugins.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Plugins</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">Why do hierarchical instruction files often outperform a single giant root file?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Rules stay close to the code they govern, reducing ambiguity in mixed-language or mixed-architecture repos</li>
<li dir="auto">Subdirectory instructions can refine local constraints without polluting global context</li>
<li dir="auto">Smaller scoped files usually improve relevance-to-token ratio in the agent prompt</li>
<li dir="auto">The tradeoff is governance complexity: teams need clear precedence and periodic cleanup<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Agent Instructions.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Agent Instructions.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agent Instructions</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">What is the fastest way to detect that an instruction file is harming agent quality?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Repeated command failures from outdated scripts or wrong paths</li>
<li dir="auto">Inconsistent code style across tasks despite explicit rules</li>
<li dir="auto">Frequent edits that violate architecture constraints the file claims to enforce</li>
<li dir="auto">Rapid fix is to trim to high-signal rules, remove contradictions, then re-add specifics incrementally<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Agent Instructions.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Agent Instructions.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agent Instructions</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div><div><div data-callout-metadata="" data-callout-fold="-" data-callout="question" class="callout is-collapsible is-collapsed node-insert-event"><div class="callout-title" dir="auto"><div class="callout-icon"><svg width="16" height="16"></svg></div><div class="callout-title-inner">When should a team keep instructions minimal and rely more on repository inspection?</div><div class="callout-fold is-collapsed"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round" class="svg-icon lucide-chevron-down"><path d="m6 9 6 6 6-6"></path></svg></div></div><div class="callout-content" style="display: none;">
<ul>
<li dir="auto">Early prototype phases where architecture and conventions are changing weekly</li>
<li dir="auto">Small codebases where implicit conventions are obvious from a few files</li>
<li dir="auto">Teams that cannot maintain rule quality yet; stale detail is worse than concise accurate guidance</li>
<li dir="auto">As complexity grows, move from minimal to layered instructions before inconsistency becomes expensive<br>
<em><a class="internal-link" data-href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Agent Instructions.md" href="Software Engineering/11 AI &amp; ML/Tooling/Agent Instructions.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow">Agent Instructions</a></em></li>
</ul>
</div></div></div>
